  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer10977320877272473873"><p><strong>"A family saga, a revenge fantasy, a <i>Twilight Zone</i>-esque parable, and, most importantly, a very fun read."Â â€”<i>Entertainment Weekly</i></strong><br/><br/>This story of two men locked in a war of wills that threatens their very existence is vintage Irvine Welsh. Troubled restaurant inspector Danny Skinner is on a quest to find the mysterious father his mother will not identify. Unraveling this hi</p></span>
  <span id="freeText10977320877272473873" style="display:none"><p><strong>"A family saga, a revenge fantasy, a <i>Twilight Zone</i>-esque parable, and, most importantly, a very fun read."Â â€”<i>Entertainment Weekly</i></strong><br /><br />This story of two men locked in a war of wills that threatens their very existence is vintage Irvine Welsh. Troubled restaurant inspector Danny Skinner is on a quest to find the mysterious father his mother will not identify. Unraveling this hidden information is the key to understanding the crippling compulsions that threaten to wreck his young life. His ensuing journey takes him from the festival city of Edinburgh to the foodie city of San Francisco. But the hard-drinking, womanizing Skinner has a strange nemesis in the form of mild-mannered fellow inspector Brian Kibby.<br /><br />It is Skinner's unfathomable, obsessive hatred of Kibby that takes over everything, threatening to destroy not only Skinner and his mission but also those he loves most dearly. When Kibby contracts a horrific, undiagnosable illness, Skinner understands that his destiny is inextricably bound to that of his hated rival, and he is faced with a terrible dilemma.<br /><br />Irvine Welsh's work is a transgressive parable about the great obsessions of our time: food, sex, and celebrity.</p></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1169698590" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1169698590" />
    <a title="JK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2058014-jk"><img alt="JK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604495956p2/2058014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1169698590?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JK" href="/user/show/2058014-jk">JK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099483588" href="/book/show/894849.The_Bedroom_Secrets_of_the_Master_Chefs">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1169698590" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9104621196169593534">The male psyche is a complex thing. In the never-ending battle to become the alpha, men beat their chests in an attempt to not only establish dominance over their brothers, but to commit the most humiliating act possible - embarrass them in front of the burds. In this way, rivalries are created and nurtured, and Welsh shows us in <i>Bedroom Secrets</i> how poisonous this rivalry can truly be.<br/><br/>Danny Skinner is a pretty standard Welsh character, with flashes of Bruce Robertson and Sick Boy peeping through</span>
  <span id="freeText9104621196169593534" style="display:none">The male psyche is a complex thing. In the never-ending battle to become the alpha, men beat their chests in an attempt to not only establish dominance over their brothers, but to commit the most humiliating act possible - embarrass them in front of the burds. In this way, rivalries are created and nurtured, and Welsh shows us in <i>Bedroom Secrets</i> how poisonous this rivalry can truly be.<br /><br />Danny Skinner is a pretty standard Welsh character, with flashes of Bruce Robertson and Sick Boy peeping through the cracks. Through booze benders, and his love of being a part-time football casual, we begin to understand his motivations. <br /><br />When mummy’s boy Brian Kibby arrives for his first day in Skinner’s work, something malicious takes root in Skinner’s mind - a hatred for which he can’t locate the source. Kibby is a hillwalker, a train-set collector, and a total geek. He struggles talking to women, his shyness holds him back in the office. There is nothing about Kibby for Skinner to feel threatened by, yet this unfathomable hatred takes root, and Skinner unknowingly casts a curse upon him which will link them inexplicably in the future.<br /><br />Welsh uses these two men to show us the horrors of this type of hatred, the similarities between them which we hadn’t quite clicked on to initially, and explores the differences in family situations. One man has grown up a product of a single parent family, and the other in a much-coveted standard mum, dad, and kids outfit. But, do either of these upbringings have any sort of effect on their moral compass? Or are you just your da’s bairn?<br /><br />None of the characters here have anything redeeming about them, and that is one of Welsh’s skills. Everyone here has their skin peeled back for us to see the maggots underneath, and I love it. <br /><br />I also particularly enjoyed Skinner’s jaunt to America, where I saw him become a better person. Is there something about living here amongst the grey, dreich, depressing buildings, the closed off, miserable people, and the complete lack of vitamin D, that drives us all to drink? Doesn’t take a genius.<br /><br />Although I’ll admit there are far better Welsh novels, this is still a wee underrated gem. You’ve got your flawed characters, you’ve got a good bit of violence, and you’ve got the booze. Fucking party.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9104621196169593534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1251517" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1251517" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/85557-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475584651p2/85557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1251517?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/85557-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Susie</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1251517" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8935446649101627588">Contrary to the old cliche', I won't say Irving Welsh has "done it again." Rather, he has gone well beyond his standard excellent story-telling and character draughtsmanship.<br/><br/>In <u>The Bedroom Securets...</u> Welsh spins a yarn so outlandish and raucous that the laughter one expects flows early and often; yet, before one expects it, the turn of events spirals into a deadly grudge match between two odious people so unsympathetic in Welsh's portrayal, it would take a masterful writer to inculcate empathy </span>
  <span id="freeText8935446649101627588" style="display:none">Contrary to the old cliche', I won't say Irving Welsh has "done it again." Rather, he has gone well beyond his standard excellent story-telling and character draughtsmanship.<br /><br />In <u>The Bedroom Securets...</u> Welsh spins a yarn so outlandish and raucous that the laughter one expects flows early and often; yet, before one expects it, the turn of events spirals into a deadly grudge match between two odious people so unsympathetic in Welsh's portrayal, it would take a masterful writer to inculcate empathy or urgency to follow their rivalry to the last page. Welsh proves that masterful, and getting up to walk in the aftermath of this novel leaves the reader weak and dizzy. But it also inspires admiration.<br /><br />What really catapults this novel above anything he has written since Marabou Stork Nightmares is not the obvious -- his tendency to capture dialect, to concoct improbable and gut busting twists of plot. Instead, Welsh joins the ranks of Henry Miller and Charles Bukowski by exposing us to the world outside our doors, unfiltered, wondrous and strange. By clearing more panes in the Doors of Perception, Welsh demonstrates quite ably why sheer charisma, restraint, virtue, or intelligence can not suffice to make a person good or achieve transcendence. For Daniel Skinner and Brian Kibby, Welsh's protagonists, this lesson comes at the expense of cherished notions and trite comforts; for the reader, the lesson proves a squirmy and unsettling one indeed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8935446649101627588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18396548851189875569" style="display:none">I love Irvine Welsh and consider him to be a sort of poet-bard for a certain broken section of society. He has a great ear and feel for dialogue. His characters are often horribly flawed, but the reader can still relate to them, sort of like that friend you no doubt have who is a bit of an asshole.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_109638052" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109638052" />
    <a title="Aaron (Typographical Era)  " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1054156-aaron-typographical-era"><img alt="Aaron (Typographical Era)  " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1384264801p2/1054156.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109638052?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron (Typographical Era)  " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron (Typographical Era)  " href="/user/show/1054156-aaron-typographical-era">Aaron (Typographical Era)  </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780393064537" href="/book/show/23959.The_Bedroom_Secrets_of_the_Master_Chefs">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1054156-aaron-typographical-era?shelf=nook">nook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1054156-aaron-typographical-era?shelf=2006">2006</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1054156-aaron-typographical-era?shelf=irvine-welsh">irvine-welsh</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109638052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16660348733045932940">(<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.opinionless.com/goodreads-i-read-in-2010-06-the-bedroom-secrets-of-the-master-chefs/'>http://www.opinionless.com/goodreads-...</a>)<br/><br/>First things first, in the interest of disclosure I must inform any one who happens to read this that I love the novels of Irvine Welsh. I’ve read just about everything the man has ever written from his 1993 debut “Trainspotting” forward and very rarely has he disappointed me.<br/><br/>That said, even if you hate Irvine Welsh, the one point you can’t argue is that man knows how to write. The main stumbling block most readers encounter when picking up his work is t</span>
  <span id="freeText16660348733045932940" style="display:none">(<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.opinionless.com/goodreads-i-read-in-2010-06-the-bedroom-secrets-of-the-master-chefs/">http://www.opinionless.com/goodreads-...</a>)<br /><br />First things first, in the interest of disclosure I must inform any one who happens to read this that I love the novels of Irvine Welsh. I’ve read just about everything the man has ever written from his 1993 debut “Trainspotting” forward and very rarely has he disappointed me.<br /><br />That said, even if you hate Irvine Welsh, the one point you can’t argue is that man knows how to write. The main stumbling block most readers encounter when picking up his work is that he tends to write the bulk of his characters’ conversations in Scottish dialect, but once you break through this hurdle his storytelling is top notch. In “Bedroom Secrets” this road block is removed for the most part, but nothing else is sacrificed in the process.<br /><br />If you’ve never read an Irvine Welsh novel before this one is great place to start. It’s a little tamer then most of his other work so it gives you a chance to ease yourself into his twisted brain and his methods of storytelling.<br /><br />Personally I picked this one up not knowing anything about it going in. For some reason its existence totally escaped me. That said, for the most part plot wise it kept me guessing until close to the very end. All of the characters felt very well fleshed out and the chefs…well the chefs were all very, ah, I’ll go with “unique” if not flat out disturbing at times.<br /><br />Welsh uses this work to tackle two topics that I found entertaining and interesting. The first is the relationship between male rivals. With this he digs in deep and the inner monologue of both of the main characters feels genuine. Welsh breaks down the male psyche and does an excellent job explaining what makes us create these rivalries, what makes us keep at them, and what causes us to need the other person so desperately in order to validate our own existence. I found myself spending the bulk of my time laughing and nodding my head “yes, yes, yes” while reading.<br /><br />The other thing he questions here is nature vs. nurture. Will a child who grows up having never met his dad grow up to be just like his old man? How much do the early moments in our development shape who we’ll become later in life? Welsh does a good job exploring both of these questions through the stories main characters.<br /><br />The only place where the novel loses points is in its very brief attempt to compare the plight of the main characters with George Bush and the Iraq war. Thankfully Welsh only spends one brief paragraph on this idea and then quickly moves along. It’s not that he’s necessarily wrong; it’s just a tough sell within the framework of this tale.<br /><br />Overall a highly entertaining read and one that felt considerably lighter in tone then the rest of Welsh’s body of work, but equally as great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16660348733045932940" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review109638052">

<div id="review_2066664532" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2066664532" />
    <a title="John Bruni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16694190-john-bruni"><img alt="John Bruni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442502006p2/16694190.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2066664532?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Bruni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Bruni" href="/user/show/16694190-john-bruni">John Bruni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2066664532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1132178969196193035">Another great book from Welsh. It took me a while to get into it, but when I realized what he was doing, I was hooked. At first I figured Danny Skinner for your average Welsh jerk, but by the end he was maybe one of my favorite characters from Welsh's work. I love the Dorian Gray aspect of this story as Skinner pushes his excess as far as it can go. I kind of guessed who his dad was early on in the book, but the journey to get there was a lot of fun. I can't recommend this book enough.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/412572501" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099483588" href="/book/show/894849.The_Bedroom_Secrets_of_the_Master_Chefs">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/997681-trisha?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/997681-trisha?shelf=hilarity">hilarity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/997681-trisha?shelf=dark">dark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/997681-trisha?shelf=i-borrowed-it">i-borrowed-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer412572501&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412572501" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8702536886814520034">The only reason I'm not giving it 5 stars is that I'll probably never re-read it again. It's not as awful and depressing as I thought it would be before reading it. The comedy of it was a great delight, even though it was awful at the same time as being funny. I've seen TRAINSPOTTING the movie, and from that I know I never want to read the book version of it. My mum tells me the book version is even more horrible than the movie, too, so that just puts the final nail in the coffin. Anyway, I read</span>
  <span id="freeText8702536886814520034" style="display:none">The only reason I'm not giving it 5 stars is that I'll probably never re-read it again. It's not as awful and depressing as I thought it would be before reading it. The comedy of it was a great delight, even though it was awful at the same time as being funny. I've seen TRAINSPOTTING the movie, and from that I know I never want to read the book version of it. My mum tells me the book version is even more horrible than the movie, too, so that just puts the final nail in the coffin. Anyway, I read this book and absolutely loved the humour, the Scottish language and the twisting turns of the plot. The way it ended still makes me laugh even though it was so tragic. I do wonder though where those who survived will go from here.<br /><br />The first moment when I actually burst out into uncontrollable laughter was the day of Brian Kibby's presentation at work, when he was feeling the first seriously bad effects of Skinner's curse on him, and how he spewed up the bacon &amp; eggs his mum had made him. I know that doesn't sound like a particularly funny moment, but the whole thing was just so absurd that it got me laughing. Prior to that, I had already been amused in a cringing sort of way by Brian's utter nerdiness, especially as it contrasted with Skinner's roguishness. Another of my favourite moments was when Brian finally got to the point where he stopped taking shit &amp; started fighting back. I loved his transformation from timid little "arselicker" to what he became, even though I probably should see that transformation as all part of the tragedy.<br /><br />I admit that for a lot of the book I hoped justice would find Skinner, and I guess it certainly did. But in the end I was hoping outright disaster would be averted. I should have known better, I guess. ;)<br /><br />All in all, this was a bleakly entertaining read, but I think the entertaining part won out in the end. Still not sure I will ever read another Irvine Welsh book though.</span>

<div id="review_660375585" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/660375585" />
    <a title="Dane Cobain" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19638210-dane-cobain"><img alt="Dane Cobain" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571333550p2/19638210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/660375585?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dane Cobain" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dane Cobain" href="/user/show/19638210-dane-cobain">Dane Cobain</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer660375585" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6608603089224794857">Welsh is back on fine form here, and while he has written better (Marabou Stork Nightmares might well be an undiscovered modern classic), this still makes for a fantastic read. And, like most of Welsh’s work, it’s difficult to stop reading it, once the story has absorbed you – in fact, I read this as part of my 24-Hour Dyslexia Action charity readathon, and reading it from cover to cover in one sitting wasn’t a problem. It’s just the kind of story that does that to you.<br/><br/>Loosely speaking, it follo</span>
  <span id="freeText6608603089224794857" style="display:none">Welsh is back on fine form here, and while he has written better (Marabou Stork Nightmares might well be an undiscovered modern classic), this still makes for a fantastic read. And, like most of Welsh’s work, it’s difficult to stop reading it, once the story has absorbed you – in fact, I read this as part of my 24-Hour Dyslexia Action charity readathon, and reading it from cover to cover in one sitting wasn’t a problem. It’s just the kind of story that does that to you.<br /><br />Loosely speaking, it follows the story of a typical Irvine Welsh character called Danny Skinner, a “hard-drinking, womanising officer” at Edinburgh’s Department of Environmental Health. When the department is joined by a calm, mild-mannered model railway enthusiast called Brian Kibby, Skinner finds himself getting agitated, treating Kibby as a nemesis even though he hasn’t really done anything except for being a living representation of everything that Skinner hates.<br /><br />Then, and with a sprinkling of style, something strange begins to happen – the two seem to share a strange link, and Kibby starts to suffer from bad hangovers and comedowns despite never touching a drop of the stuff, while Skinner can hit the town with no ill effects the morning after. It’s almost like black slapstick, like black humour – humour about dark subjects like death and mental illness, as opposed to humour by, for and about black people. I’m not a racist, I promise.<br /><br />And, as usual, Welsh writes in his traditional style, which includes plenty of Scots dialect, plenty of drinking, drug use and debauchery, and his use of profanities and an indented hyphen to signify dialogue. – Kinda like this ya radge c**t.<br /><br />This can occasionally make it difficult to follow the story, and your brain can start processing narrative as dialogue and vice versa – that said, Welsh’s writing tends to flow from one paragraph to another, and so the odds are that if you have to concentrate to understand something, it’s probably intentional.<br /><br />And that’s one of the things with Welsh’s writing – it grows on you, and you can re-read his stories and see subtle undertones that weren’t there on your first read. I’ve only read this novel once, and so I’m probably not the fairest of judges, but I have a feeling that that’s the case here, too. Perhaps I’ll re-read it myself at some point, but I need to finish the rest of Welsh’s books first. So the real question is, fancy a race?</span>
  <a data-text-id="6608603089224794857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2640695-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1257395272p2/2640695.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780393064537" href="/book/show/23959.The_Bedroom_Secrets_of_the_Master_Chefs">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84345168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7695951544822297878">A brilliantly entertaining read. Welsh, the author of "Trainspotting," who was raised in a Scottish housing project, waxes poignantly and believably on life in Edinburgh as seen through the eyes of several mid to late twentysomethings, most notably "Skinner," who is the protagonist.<br/><br/>Raised by a "punk-rock" mother, Skinner, (conceived in the blitzkreig of her wild and crazy youth) is on a quest to locate his father, whom is evidenced to still be living, but could be any one of several different pe</span>
  <span id="freeText7695951544822297878" style="display:none">A brilliantly entertaining read. Welsh, the author of "Trainspotting," who was raised in a Scottish housing project, waxes poignantly and believably on life in Edinburgh as seen through the eyes of several mid to late twentysomethings, most notably "Skinner," who is the protagonist.<br /><br />Raised by a "punk-rock" mother, Skinner, (conceived in the blitzkreig of her wild and crazy youth) is on a quest to locate his father, whom is evidenced to still be living, but could be any one of several different people. Nonchalantly successful at winning the adoration of women, he lives a broodish lifetyle of pub lurking and socializing with his mates. <br /><br />The main event of the book takes place after he is refused by his fiancee, due to his excessively indulgent drinking habits. Bereft, and seething, he decides that me must go to San Francisco in order to meet a fellow living there whom his Mum claims may be his Dad. While there, he attempts to reform his alcoholism, and falls in love with a girl that he meets at an AA meeting.<br /><br />Once back in Edinburgh, he accidentally and quite suddenly finds himself falling for yet another girl. This time she is the sister of a self-righteous reject, who has lately succumbed to a debilitating illness. This fellow, Kibby, is suspicious and jealous of Skinner's intentions. Have no sexual or social prowess of his own, he eagerly self-deprecates, all the while subtly plotting a way to get vengeance. <br /><br />After Skinner is accepted graciously into the familial fold of his new love, it occurs to him that his true love is the woman that he met back in the states. As his new girlfriend grows increasingly needy and desperate in his eyes, he finds her less and less sexy. Ultimately he resigns that he shall return to the states and commence a romantic co-habitation with his former flame from AA. <br /><br />I wont betray the ending, but it is not a happy one. The strongest aspects of the story is it's raw and unembellished portrayal of the characters, and their city. A very contemporary glimpse into the darker urban underbelly this world region.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7695951544822297878" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1267313934" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1267313934" />
    <a title="Logan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/379310-logan"><img alt="Logan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592576542p2/379310.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1267313934?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Logan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Logan" href="/user/show/379310-logan">Logan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1267313934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17003694742445395765">First off, this is NOT about the sex lives of chefs, which I was very glad to discover. To me, the title actually made me not want to read this book, tho I know the whole reason this book has this title is to draw readers in. Are there chefs in this book? Is there sex in this book? Yes, to both. But, no, it is not "about" the bedroom secrets of master chefs. And chefs aren't the main characters.<br/><br/>Now that that is out of the way... what this book really is is an allegory. It's about alcoholism, abu</span>
  <span id="freeText17003694742445395765" style="display:none">First off, this is NOT about the sex lives of chefs, which I was very glad to discover. To me, the title actually made me not want to read this book, tho I know the whole reason this book has this title is to draw readers in. Are there chefs in this book? Is there sex in this book? Yes, to both. But, no, it is not "about" the bedroom secrets of master chefs. And chefs aren't the main characters.<br /><br />Now that that is out of the way... what this book really is is an allegory. It's about alcoholism, abuse, self-abuse, family, and lies. It's also about repression and over-indulgence. As is the case with many of Welshy's books since the aughts, it's pretty much an epic as it spans a great deal of activity (and some time) and fills a good number of pages. How much heavy lifting is needed is probably relative to the reader.<br /><br />I guess I'm making that statement because I do think it could have been shorter. But I am also aware I say that about a ton of books, so it could just be my own predilection. <br /><br />And it's conflicting, because I still enjoyed this book through and through. There was a really odd, somewhat unnerving Kafka-like idea behind this book in the way it presented its allegory--a violent, angry, self-defeating, depressed action to the characters' motives that was really intriguing and engaging. And it was a bit mystical, allowing a bit of fantasy into the reality of this story. <br /><br />I dug that a lot.<br /><br />Good read. I realize, too, that I judge these books against his others, so if this story came from someone else (and, really, how could it?) I might be doing more cartwheels over it. But when you have MARABOU STORK NIGHTMARE, FILTH, and TRAINSPOTTING already, it can make the other books seem a bit pale in comparison.<br /><br />How fair is that? Life ain't fair.<br /><br />SAME RULES APPLY!<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17003694742445395765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2275838891583899159">*Re-Read* I remember enjoying this book the first time and still enjoyed it second time round. It may not live up to the greatness of his best books or my personal favourites. But it is still a great Irvine Welsh book. This one has more fantasy elements than a lot of his work, more along the lines of some of the stories in 'The Acid House' still it makes for an interesting story and as I am leaving this without spoilers I will not say anything. The. Book made me laugh, some good gross out moment</span>
  <span id="freeText2275838891583899159" style="display:none">*Re-Read* I remember enjoying this book the first time and still enjoyed it second time round. It may not live up to the greatness of his best books or my personal favourites. But it is still a great Irvine Welsh book. This one has more fantasy elements than a lot of his work, more along the lines of some of the stories in 'The Acid House' still it makes for an interesting story and as I am leaving this without spoilers I will not say anything. The. Book made me laugh, some good gross out moments that Irvine Welsh can excel at, he writes a great disgusting sex scene. Part of me can see why some people do not rate this book as highly as some of his work and part of me can see that. But to me it is still 5Stars, he is one of my favourite authors and I enjoyed reading this again. If you are a fan it is at least worth a try. &amp; if you are like me, more Irvine Welsh is never a bad thing.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11566349312802320523">With <i>Ulysses</i> done, I need to read something fast, entertaining, pulpy. Thanks, Irvine Welsh.<br/><br/>This brow-leaping habit may be genetic. My mom apparently read <i>Salem's Lot</i> (King) right after <i>Anna Karenina</i> (Tolstoy).<br/><br/>...<br/><br/>Finished. Enjoyable, but a bunch of issues make this a clumsier Welsh effort overall:<br/>1. Guessed crux of climactic plot reveal in first 100 pages, and sort of knew how things would end.<br/>2.   <span id="freeText11566349312802320523" style="display:none">With <i>Ulysses</i> done, I need to read something fast, entertaining, pulpy. Thanks, Irvine Welsh.<br /><br />This brow-leaping habit may be genetic. My mom apparently read <i>Salem's Lot</i> (King) right after <i>Anna Karenina</i> (Tolstoy).<br /><br />...<br /><br />Finished. Enjoyable, but a bunch of issues make this a clumsier Welsh effort overall:<br />1. Guessed crux of climactic plot reveal in first 100 pages, and sort of knew how things would end.<br />2. There were likeable characters, but not so much the main protagonists/antagonists (okay, hardly unique for Welsh, but <i>Glue</i> was so human that I hope for more now.<br />3. Inconsistent perspective never justified in any reasonable way.<br />4. Felt need to spell out the theme not once but twice, where tangential evidence had already sufficed.<br />5. Dialect toned down in a manner that makes me suspect Welsh was shooting for wider readability. But I've always enjoyed the dialect.<br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2663332026798834349">YES! After reading a few crapfests recently (not by this author) I am happy to report Irvine Welsh rocks me yet again.<br/><br/>Whenever I read Welsh I can't help but adapt his words to film in my mind. He writes really lush characters and settings that ordinarily aren't extraordinary - but he makes them so. <br/><br/><br/><br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3428471195" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3428471195" />
    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3428471195?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3428471195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3592459991043704561">More like 3.5 stars.<br/><br/>*MILD SPOILERS*<br/>Straying away from his usual formula of Leith-bound junkies and sadistic criminality, Welsh brings us something a little different that works a little more than half the time. Danny Skinner is a alcoholic in his early-20s who artfully dodges his way through work and relationships. He is not a very nice person, a typical Welsh (I think of it as anti-Dickens, to place him in the British literary continuum) anti-hero whose success derives from his despicable natur</span>
  <span id="freeText3592459991043704561" style="display:none">More like 3.5 stars.<br /><br />*MILD SPOILERS*<br />Straying away from his usual formula of Leith-bound junkies and sadistic criminality, Welsh brings us something a little different that works a little more than half the time. Danny Skinner is a alcoholic in his early-20s who artfully dodges his way through work and relationships. He is not a very nice person, a typical Welsh (I think of it as anti-Dickens, to place him in the British literary continuum) anti-hero whose success derives from his despicable nature. Versus Skinner is Brian Kibby, a nerdy, model-train aficionado. From the get-go, the two don't get on very well, especially when Skinner discovers that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[whatever he does to his body (drugs, alcohol, sodomy) appears on Kibby's body, not his. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> You'll likely figure out what's going on about halfway through the novel, but somehow its predictability doesn't affect its worth.<br />I'll leave it at that. There are moments of flaring genius. There is a running and too rare theme of the evils of the proxy or surrogate. The novel takes place during the war in Iraq and the idea of realizing your evils by using others is stark and clear, but Welsh doesn't give us enough of that. He always shines when he turns his shit-smeared lens onto our society and culture. Here he tends to focus more on the individuals, more so than in other novels, with mixed results. It's still a fun read and when it gets more twisted and surreal, the novel really thrives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3592459991043704561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_126936766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/126936766" />
    <a title="Pvw" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4377693-pvw"><img alt="Pvw" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291127389p2/4377693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/126936766?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pvw" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pvw" href="/user/show/4377693-pvw">Pvw</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer126936766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9218262454290227135">A modern update of "Dorian Gray", in which the poor victim that suffers all the traces of the protagonist's abusive drinking, drugs and sex is not a painting, but a real life colleague of his. In the beginning, our macho restaurant critic utterly resents the shy and boring Brian Kibby, who always gets promoted in front of him, because he himself suffers from a major drinking problem and Kibby is like decency incarnated. One night he can't take it any more and curses Kibby - after which he furthe</span>
  <span id="freeText9218262454290227135" style="display:none">A modern update of "Dorian Gray", in which the poor victim that suffers all the traces of the protagonist's abusive drinking, drugs and sex is not a painting, but a real life colleague of his. In the beginning, our macho restaurant critic utterly resents the shy and boring Brian Kibby, who always gets promoted in front of him, because he himself suffers from a major drinking problem and Kibby is like decency incarnated. One night he can't take it any more and curses Kibby - after which he further drowns his misery in booze. The next day, he not only doesn't have the usual hangover, but Kibby shows up late for work and looks like a drunk bum. From then on, our restaurant critic abuses the strange physical connection between him and Kibby, only taking just enough care as not to kill him right away.<br /><br />While I wasn't wild about the book while reading it, I must say the story keeps holding a grip on you later on. Welsh has managed to invent complex characters and convey a claustrophobic atmosphere in which lust, hatred, agression and self-destructiveness all get mixed up. Apart from that, the novel has got quite enough surprises up its sleeve to be entertaining until the end. Not as good as "Trainspotting", but certainly worth a read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9218262454290227135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1309336291" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<div id="review_2834153846" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2834153846" />
    <a title="April" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24591006-april"><img alt="April" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1381369884p2/24591006.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2834153846?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="April" class="user" itemprop="url" name="April" href="/user/show/24591006-april">April</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2834153846&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2834153846" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2623894086129324855">This book was part of Derek T's collection that he was getting rid of during a move. He drove up a huge box of books for me from LA and this copy was in there--beaten up with a strip of black duct tape holding together the spine. How did it get that way? Was it read over and over or something, like a favorite book? Or did Derek find it? Or was it even his and did he even read it? I'll have to ask Derek.<br/><br/>A very intense read, as I suppose his other books are too. I've only read <i>Trainspotting</i> and th</span>
  <span id="freeText2623894086129324855" style="display:none">This book was part of Derek T's collection that he was getting rid of during a move. He drove up a huge box of books for me from LA and this copy was in there--beaten up with a strip of black duct tape holding together the spine. How did it get that way? Was it read over and over or something, like a favorite book? Or did Derek find it? Or was it even his and did he even read it? I'll have to ask Derek.<br /><br />A very intense read, as I suppose his other books are too. I've only read <i>Trainspotting</i> and that was intense in a different way (all the drug usage and its consequences, the disgusting predicaments, and violence; although I suppose there was a bit of violence and disgusting predicaments in this book too). He leads us on to think along the same lines as Skinner as he searches for his father, for example with the vignette of Greg Tomlin looking at an old photo. <br /><br />Something kept throwing me off about Skinner--the fact that he was referred to as "Skinner" made me think of an older guy, like maybe in his mid-30s and a little washed out, instead of a younger, strapping, handsome fellow. (Probably due to an American TV reference.) So I kept thinking of this washed out character in his mid-30s doing all these crazy alcohol-fueled antics and having the thoughts and sex he had. I don't know, I just thought of him at that age through the whole book and kept needing the reminders that he was this strapping young guy just a couple years out of Uni. Anyway, that's mostly besides the point. <br /><br />The idea that someone could shift their physical deterioration to someone else was a new storyline concept to me and I found it interesting to see how it played out. The narrative perspective shifts were also an interesting call. I wasn't sure if it was necessary to do that, but I could see how it added interest and helped move the story along. <br /><br />I wonder if it had to end in one character's annihilation? Why couldn't they both continue to exist? And even maybe build a meaningful relationship together? I guess the ending had some vindication in some ways, but it also left me feeling empty and sad that something more meaningful couldn't be built from the previously misdirected or misplaced hatred that was meted out. I wanted reconciliation and a "Wow, so that's what was going on between us! I'm so glad we figured out this mystery. How crazy is all that??" I guess I was looking for a happier ending, but not all endings get to be happy.<br /><br />"But when Kay did enter the flat there was a distance about her and a resolve that he'd never seen before. And at that point a dagger seemed to twist deep inside him and he guessed that it was over before she even opened her mouth. <br />And as if on cue she delivered the words, -- It's over, Danny, with a stark, uncompromising air of finality.<br />Skinner was crushed by her words. He wished he wasn't but he was. He felt something real, something essential die inside him; felt it actually leave his body. It was a rich, deep and vital energy, a cardinal component of the self. Stricken, he wondered if he would ever get it back, or if that's what life was to be: steady erosion followed by an occasional big subsidence. He was surely too young to feel like this. His anguished gasp was deep, disturbing and primal, shocking both him and Kay." pg. 90<br /><br />"The early-morning hours slowly faded away, just as the night had slipped by in a sleepless agony. Kibby's swollen eyes ached and itched full of sleeplessness's phantom dirt. He squirmed like a beached porpoise on his sweat-soaked bed." pg. 149<br /><br />"Poor Big Rab, suffering from cirrhosis of the liver, now never able to drink again. What a nightmare. That's the problem with intoxication, it's an immediate state, belonging in the present. You can't live your life on the memory of previous consumption." pg. 220<br /><br />"Skinner realised that he'd seldom thought about a woman in purely sexual terms. Even if somebody was an object of his desire, he invariably found himself thinking about her level of intelligence, music, clothing, film and book preferences, the sort of friends she had, her social and political views, what her parents did for a living. Yes, he had got involved in one-night stands, but casual relationships were always unsatisfying to him. He looked searchingly at Kibby. -- I'm just interested in women, Brian." pg. 379 <br /><br />Book: from Derek T's collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2623894086129324855" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2072931083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2072931083" />
    <a title="1000rpm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14332377-1000rpm"><img alt="1000rpm" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2072931083?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="1000rpm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="1000rpm" href="/user/show/14332377-1000rpm">1000rpm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2072931083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15247923415169647224">Another quite nasty Irvine Welsh Novel. I made the mistake of taking it on a family holiday and it proved quite a contrast to the rest of the holiday.<br/><br/>This is somewhat a departure for Mr Welsh. Usually he revels in the horrible antics of the druggy underclass (and Policemen) of Edinburgh, like Trainspotting, Glue, Ecstacy, etc. And this is pretty much the same, however there is a large fantasy element in the addition of a kind of real world re-imagining of The Picture of Dorian Gray, which is ref</span>
  <span id="freeText15247923415169647224" style="display:none">Another quite nasty Irvine Welsh Novel. I made the mistake of taking it on a family holiday and it proved quite a contrast to the rest of the holiday.<br /><br />This is somewhat a departure for Mr Welsh. Usually he revels in the horrible antics of the druggy underclass (and Policemen) of Edinburgh, like Trainspotting, Glue, Ecstacy, etc. And this is pretty much the same, however there is a large fantasy element in the addition of a kind of real world re-imagining of The Picture of Dorian Gray, which is referred to in the book. I remember some of his fantasy stuff in his Acid House short stories - but that could have been just drug-trips.<br /><br />Most of the book surrounds the misadventures of two environmental health officers from Edinburgh and the womanising and alcoholism of one of them against a background of him searching for his unknown father. There are some horrible scenes, but some strangely uplifting elements as well. I'm not sure I'd recommend it to anyone without knowing them well. You have to be a funny kind of person to like his books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15247923415169647224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3229879834" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3229879834" />
    <a title="Gavin Spillane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/107019721-gavin-spillane"><img alt="Gavin Spillane" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3229879834?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gavin Spillane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gavin Spillane" href="/user/show/107019721-gavin-spillane">Gavin Spillane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3229879834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5639156397765262843">This is my first time reading Irvine Welsh and Ill be sure to read more of his books in the future.<br/>The title of the book has little to do with the plot, I was glad to discover when introduced to the protagonist Danny Skinner who has an irrational hatred for his coworker Brian Kibby.<br/>I thought that while Danny rarely veers from his arrogance and malevolent thoughts throughout, we see glimpses of vulnerability through his interest in scandinavian poetry and love for (multiple) members of the opposi</span>
  <span id="freeText5639156397765262843" style="display:none">This is my first time reading Irvine Welsh and Ill be sure to read more of his books in the future.<br />The title of the book has little to do with the plot, I was glad to discover when introduced to the protagonist Danny Skinner who has an irrational hatred for his coworker Brian Kibby.<br />I thought that while Danny rarely veers from his arrogance and malevolent thoughts throughout, we see glimpses of vulnerability through his interest in scandinavian poetry and love for (multiple) members of the opposite sex. <br />Male rivalries can be dangerous and destructive enough, but when the weight of hangovers and comedowns are by some paranormal force taken off Dannys shoulders, this gives him the greenlight to descend further into hedonistic madness. Of course somebody has to suffer the consequences and that somebody is the straight edge Brian who is crushed by this hex inflicted by his nemesis. <br />Colorful imagery, acerbic Scottish humour and the supernatural are all consistencies in the story and help to set up a memorable twist at the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5639156397765262843" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FtAEgELeK1SZ04KOl8T8srQKItLBGsd+A7MknT1USPcs9m1S+YDiPD74Nu9gMfZtYlXFGg+XSRlYzWIs8Be6bQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

In secret passageways, hidden rooms, and the troubled mind of our narrator, a mystery perpetually takes shape—and the most compelling clue to its final nature is “the marbled swarm” itself, a complex amalgam of language passed down from father to son.\n\nCooper ensnares the reader in a world of appearances, where the trappings of high art, old money, and haute cuisine obscure an unspeakable system of coercion and surrender.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5687.Irvine_Welsh"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1461084249p3/5687.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1405445915494065184">Probably most famous for his gritty depiction of a gang of Scottish Heroin addicts, <i>Trainspotting</i> (1993), Welsh focuses on the darker side of human nature and drug use. All of his novels are set in his native Scotland and filled with anti-heroes, small time crooks and hooligans. Welsh manages, however to imbue these characters with a sad humanity that makes them likable despite their obvious scumb</span>
  <span id="freeText1405445915494065184" style="display:none">Probably most famous for his gritty depiction of a gang of Scottish Heroin addicts, <i>Trainspotting</i> (1993), Welsh focuses on the darker side of human nature and drug use. All of his novels are set in his native Scotland and filled with anti-heroes, small time crooks and hooligans. Welsh manages, however to imbue these characters with a sad humanity that makes them likable despite their obvious scumbaggerry. Irvine Welsh is also known for writing in his native Edinburgh Scots dialect, making his prose challenging for the average reader unfamiliar with this style.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1405445915494065184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
